another study in this series . 2/ 
HOI-leVer, there were some events and

Phas e II forces .

Upon his return from Saigon, Secretary Ncl'Tamara drafted a 
MemorandQm for the President, outlining the changed military situation 
in Vietnam, and commenting that in view of the communist build-up, 
lithe presently contemplated Phase I forces will not be enough ... Nor 
i-rill the originally contemplated Phase II addition of 28 more U. S. 
battalj,ons (112,000 men ) be enough .. . Indeed it is estimated that, with 
the contemplated Phase II addition of 28 U. S. battalions 'I"e woul d be 
able only to hold our present geographical positions . II 39/ 
In order to IIprovide what it takes in men and materiel. .. t o stick 
Hith our stated objectives and i-rith the war, II Secretary McNamara recom-
mended the deployment of one Korean division plus a~other brigade, an 
additional Australian battalion, and 40 u.s. combat battalions, bringing 
the total of U. S. maneuver battalions to 74, and the total of U. S. per-
sonnel in Vietnam to approximately 400, 000 by the end of 1966 Hi th the 
possible need for an additional 200,000 in 1967 . 
LD the 7 December version of his Memorandu~ for the President, 
McNamara added the information that lIalthough the 1966 deployments to 
South Vietnam may require some shift of forces from other theaters, it 
i s believed that they can be accomplished 'l-Tithout calling up reserve 
personnel; hOl"ever , the Joint Chiefs of Staff do not believe additional 
forces can be detloyed to Southeast Asia or else;;.rhere ill11ess reserves 
are called . II 40/ 
In evaluating this course of action, the Secretary '\-larned that i t 
IIwill not guarantee success . II 
He estimated the odds to be about even 
t hat the N'ITA/VC will match the U. S. buildup and that lIeven 'I"ith the 
recommended deployments, we will be faced in early 1967 'with a military 
standoff at a much higher l evel, with pacification still stalled, and 
i-rith any prospect of military success marred by the chance of an active 
Chinese intervention . II

4. 
Results of the CINCPAC Planning Conference 
. 
On 12 February, the results of the CINCPAC Conference were 
published . 46/ 
The concept of operations for 1966 had been more completely 
spelled out. 
The three basic military objectives had by thi s time grown 
to four. 
Now there were two separate obj ectives, 
1. 
To extend GVN dominion, direction, and control over 
SVN, and 
2. 
To defeat the VC and PAVN forces in lLRVN and forc e 
their withdrawal,

The question of i-lhere the numbers for Phases I I, I IA, and IIA(R) 
came from provokes much speculation . 
It can be hypothesized that from 
the outset of the American build-up, some military men f elt that \-Tinning 
a meaningful military victory in Vietnam IoTOuld require something on the 
order of one milli on men . 
Knol'Ting that this vTOuld be unacceptable 
poliU.cally, it 'may have seemed a better bargaining strategy to ask for 
increased deployments incr8nentally . 
At the outset, the limiting factor 
on the build-up ioTas the speed 'Nith which units could be readied for 
deployment, and the speed \-Tith i'lhich logistj.cal support facilities could 
be provided in Vietnam (the later constraint being heavDy i nfluenced by

the scarcity of dock facilities and the shipping jam up in Saigon) .

t hese proble~s had been surmounted) the barrier then became the level at

This barrier became very 
r eal i n earl y ' 66 'Then General Hestmorel and ' s desires for numbers of men 
and rates of depl oyments began to exceed the capabilities of t he services

In this ,speculative explana-

II. 
PROGP~M No . 3 
As far as the actual conduct of ground operations in Vietnam was 
concerned) the period of time from 1 May 1965 to 1 November 1965 was 
spent in buj.lding up combat and logistical forces and learning to employ 
them effectively . 
This was followed by a period from 1 November 1965 to 
1 May 1966) in which the deployment of U. S. forces was extended tO,vard 
the frontiers ) logistical support vTas exercised in furnishing support to 
troops in sustained combat) and commanders were indoctrinated on the 
techniques of sustaj.ned ground combat . 
The NVA/VC avoided initiating actions which might result in 
large and unacceptable casualties from the firepower of Allied 
forc es . 
During the year the enemy became increasingly cautious 
in the face of increased Allied strength . 
The enemy tended to 
attack only 'Then he had overvThelming superiority of numbers ) 
such as during the attack in March on the Special Forces outpost 
at A Shau . 
VC tactics ,-Tere designed to conserve main force 
strengths for the most opportune targets. 
The ~WA/VC avoided 
attacking large Allied units of regiment or brigade size) but 
did attack isolated battalions and companies using sufficient 
strength to insure great numerical superiority . 
It was typical 
of th~ ener~ to attack with one-third of his available force 
and to employ the remaining tiw-thirds of the units to set up 
an ambush of the Allied relief column . 
During attacks the 
NVA/VC used a hugging tactic as a means of protecting them-
selves from Allied artillery and air strikes . 
The enemy often 
withdrew' by small squad-sized increments) using multiple routes . 
To defend against surveillance and artillery and air strikes) 
the enemy dispersed into the jungle in smail units) moved fre-
quently) and made maximum use of darkness and periods of Iml 
vis ib ili ty . . . 
~/ 
It is interesting to note) however) the pattern formed by VillCV ' s 
operations during 1966. 
In the I Corps area) the large-scale operations 
conducted by the Marines in the spring of the year were for the most 
part located along the coast of the southern part of the area) in the 
Provinces of Quang Tin and Quang Ngai. 
Beginning vr.ith Operation DOlJ13LE EAGLE I (28 January to 17 February); 
they progressed through DOUBLE EAGLE II (19 February to 1 March); 
Operation UTAH (4 March); Operation TEXAS (18 March); and Operation 
HOT SPRIl'TGS 
on 21 April. 
All of these operations \-Tere keyed on intelli-
gence of an enemy build-up i n and around Quang Ngai .

Containment of Enemy Forces in Sanctuaries 
1. 
The threat to South Vietnam of large enemy forces in 
the sanctuar'ies of Laos, Cambodia and North Vietnam has nO"T 
clearly emerged and is of increasing concern to me .

and Song Be. 
He are . therefore compelled to seel~ ways of con-

a major ingress of these forces in South Vietnam. 
2. 
The problem is now under active study by my staff. 
Redeployment of avail able forces to counter this threat may 
be necessary and could seriously j eopardize other important 
undertakings. 
Moreover, additional forces already requested 
may not be sufficient to contain the enemy forces in their 
sanctuaries and still accomplish other essential tasks .

Lodge's Attention Turns to Inflation 
VJhile General Westmoreland ' s attention "las being increasingly 
dra"m towards the problems of infiltration from sanctuaries outside the 
borders of Vietnam, Ambassador Lodge ' s attention \Vas being increasingly 
dra,.;D to'dards the problem of inflation inside the borders. 
As Ambassador 
Porter in Saigon ,vrote to Komer on 17 August : 
Fiscal year 1966 ,vas a year of inflation . 
Money suppl y 
r ose by 72% and Saigon ,,70rking class cost of living index by 
92%. Near of end of year (June 18) the piaster was devalued 
from 60 piasters per dollar to 118 piasters per dollar and 
six weeks later at time of \'lriting, prices had begun to 
stabilize .... It appears at this writing (Aug 11, 1966) that 
devaluation of Ju~e 18 has been successful surgical operation. 
It has increased by nearly 100% the number of piasters with-
drawn from circulation for each dollar of imports, and this 
has sopped up enough demand to stabilize prices and actually 
r educe the total monetary circulation. 
Retail price indices 
have sho,'ln little change for last five weeks. 
Black market 
price of green dolla.rs appears to ha.ve l evelled off at a 
level of about 185-195 , and price of gol d also declining . 
There remain, hQlvever, number of threats to this neI-Tly estab-
lished and so far fragile stability . 38/ 
He then l isted five primary threa.ts : 
The first was wage stability. 
There 
had been a general round of wage increases since devaluation, but it \Vas 
not yet certain that labor demands had been satisfied. 
Second 
\Vas mounting U.S. expenditure : 
US military build-up has t endency to generate continu-
ously greater piaster expenditure , both by US DOD officially, 
and by Our troops as individuals . 
Current total rate of 
expenditure ·a.round 36 billion piasters a year . 
In US, DOD 
programming rise to r ate of over 2 ~7 billion piasters was orig-
i nally foreseen for fiscal year 1967. 
This order of increase 
\Vould tend very definitely to upset the stabilization effort . 
Budget of 36 billion piasters for total DOD generated expen-
di ture in FY 1967 has nOvl been ordel'ed, but this may prove 
very difficult to implement. 
The third danger vias seen to be an incYf:,ased GVN budget . 
The 
total GVN civil and milital'y expenditures vrere about 55 billion piasters 
. in FY 1966, and they might rise to 70 billion Or more in FY 67 .

He ended up ~y straddling the fence . 
He stated that he believed that we should "bring as massive an American 
military force to bear in Vietnam that we can and that we should do so 
as quickly as liTe can. " 
But he hedged by adding" so long as this can be 
done without a \vildcat inflation and other lethal political effects ." 
He seemed to think he had found a solution in Westmoreland ' s ne,'T fasci -
nation vTi th the sanctuaries across the borders of South Vietnam. 
He 
hOlled that with large numbers of troops employed in the less populated 
areas, it might be possible to have both the massive force quickly 
emIlloyed and a relatively small inflationary effect . 
However , he seems 
to have been misjudging I'That liTestmoreland had in mind .

Today, with the US/FW forces available, large

However: 'with the enemy's

increase his combat strength in SVN to 131,200, approxi -
mately 7 combat divisions . It is estimated that he ,·Till 
have a combat strength of 147,300 consisting of 181 Inf 
Bns and 63 Combat spt Bns, or approximately 10 Combat

could be substantially increased . If the enemy adopts

US/FW strength in SVN may be required over requested 
1967 force levels . 
D. 
The CY 66 US/FW force increases will 80110'\'1 
tactical 
cow~anders to step up their search and destroy 
and other offensive operations both in size and frequency. 
This increase is necessary to turn the tide of the enemy 
buildup. 
The estimated enemy attrition made possible by 
this force increase ",ould hold the enemy buildup to 
approximately 147,300 combat strength as stated above . 
If the US/Nif forces continue attrition of the enemy at 
the same increasing rate during the next 12 month period 
as accomplished du.ring Jan-Jul 66, the enemy combat strength 
should start to decline during the second quarter CY 67. 
HOvTever , if the enemy accelerates buildup in SVN to his 
maximum capability, his strength probably will not start 
to decline until some time in CY 68. 
The enemy continues 
to sho\v every inclination to continue his military efforts. 
E.

a large scale forced deferral of troop incree,ses at this 
time , while the enemy continues to build up, would be a 
most imprudent course of action that could jeopardize

It is true that the majority of IIhard corell captives and defectors 
cited in the W~ND reports no longer predict an inevitable VC 
victory, many of this selected group nO,,7 see the war as a stale-
mate with each side building up its res~ect ive force . 
Although 
some of this group now see defeat, in the main the confidence of 
the individual enemy soldier in a military victory has dwindled 
due, i n l arge measure, to the string of defeats he has suffered 
at the hands of the US/GVN/ Free World Forces . 
However , limita-
tion of these US/ GVN forces for economic reasons would curtail the 
momentum of the military effort at this critical point and con-
ceivably jeopardize the overall US effort in Vietnam. 
4. 
Para 10 & 11, Part C, Ref A discusses troop utilization 
but does not depict clearly the military concept of operations in 
Vietnam for CY 67. 
Our concept recognizes and is built around 
t wo equally important , continuing and compl ementary r equirernents 
which call for the same type of military resources and flexibility 
in their application. 
On the one hand, ,,7e must maintain the 
security of our bases and key population and food producing centers 
and assist in expanding security of areas under Government control . 
On the other hand ,,7e must seek out and destroy the enemy ' s main 
forces and his bases to create the environment in which meaningful 
Revolutionary Development can proceed. 
The priority of US/YW 
military efforts will continue to be devoted to our main mission, 
the destruction of enemy main forces and bases. 
The "entirely 
new kind of ,,70rkll referred to by the Ambassador is in reality a 
continuation of our surveillance and rapid r eaction tactics vis -
a-vis enemy forces occupying sanctuaries in adjacent territory. 
We 
are according heightened emphasis to this effort , and may find it 
necessary to ask for additional forces to insure its success.

In this memorandum, McNamara reveals i'Ti th striking clarity that many 
' of the premises under Hhich the war to that point had been fought (and 
manned ) i'Tere shifting . 
He agreed with COMUSMACV that the military situation has gone 
!!somewhat better in 1966 than anticipated, !! but he found little cause 
. for optimism in the longer run . 
In fact, he seemed almost disheartened 
as he noted that there was !!no reasonable Hay to bring the war to an 
end soon." Finding an injured but undismayed opponent committed no\v to 
''Ivai ting us out !! 'while sapping our national will and seeing "pacification 
a basic disappointment ..• no better, and if anything worse off ... " hardly 
was the kind of progress he hoped for . 
His solution vTaS to get ourselves into "a military posture tha,t ,ve 
credibly i'lould maintain indefinitely -- a posture that makes trying to 
'wait us out' less attractive. " 
To do this , he proposed a five part 
program : 
(1) First, he suggested that, barring a major cha,nge in the 
war, vTe should stabilize U.S. force levels in Vietnam at about 470,000 . 
The nevI figure of 470,000 for U. S. force levels (only 25,000 above the 
l atest figure of 445,000 for Program #3 ) apparently i'laS arrived at during 
the sessions in Saigon . 
Before the meetings , VJestmoreland had estj.mated 
t hat Program 3 Hou.ld entail'a piaster cost of 47 . 4 billion . 
The folloH-up 
papers to the conference all continued to focus upon the piaster costs of 
various troop deployments with the intent to keep them under the ~.2 billion 
Lodge ceil ing. 
The most probable explanation of the genesis of the 470,000 
figur e is that it r epresented the best guess at the time of the Saigon 
meeting of "That strength could be supported Ivi thin the 42 billion limit 
by making very strong efforts to reduce piaster costs per man . 
(2) 
He recommended a barrier near the DMZ and !!across the 
trails of Laos .!! 
(3) 
He opposed expansion of the ROLLING T1IDNDER program, r ecom-
mending instead a !!stabilization!! to prevent the unsettling escalations 
from complicating our political situation (and negotiating posture ) and 
distracting from the main job of pacification. 
(4) 
He said vTe should !!pursue a vigorous pacification program" 
noting that !!progress in pacification more than anything else , will 
persuadE the enemy to negotiate or ,vithdraVT !! 51/ 
(5) 
Finally, he proferred a three-sided attempt to get negoti -
ations going by (a) shifting the pattern of our bombing (or perhaps even 
stopping it); (b) considering strategies designed to enhance the probability 
of a split beti-Teen the VC and Hanoi ; and (c) !!developing a realistic plan 
providing a role for the VC in negotiations, post"Tar life, and the govern-
ment of the nation . "

t he crux of disagreement : 
. . . . As in past concepts, it goes beyond certain 
r estraints that have been placed on US operating forces 
to date, such as those on the air campaign in North Viet -
nam, on cross border operations, 6n certain special opera-
tions, and on ground actions in the southern half of the 
demilitarized zone . 
Further, this concept shoul d be carried 
out in its entirety, if achievement of US objectives is to 
be accomplished in the shortest time and at the least cost 
i n men and materiel. 
The concept describes preparation for 
operations that have not as yet been authorized , such as 
mining ports, naval quarantine, spoiling attacks and raids 
against the enemy in Cambodia and Laos, and certain special 
operations . 
Such action will support intensified and 
accelerated revolutionary development and nation building 
programs . 
Since the forc e requirements are based on this 
concept in its entirety, continued restraints and the 
absence of authorization for r ecommended operations could 
generate significantly different requirements for forces 
and timing. §]) 
In a sense, it embraced all of the right argUI(,ents (for II intensified 
and accelerated revolutionary development and programs
ll and II shortest 
t ime at the least cost, 1I an overdetermined test) but unfortunately for 
all the wrong reasons .

militarily enchanted ,vith a costly me.jor force increase at that time , 
nor 'vith cross border and air operations whi.ch ran grave political risks .

t emporarily erased by an ambiguous statement ack..YlOi·rledging that II capability 
to meet these requirements cannot be developed without significant modifi -

policy, no call-up of reserves, m3,intenance of COTIJUS training base .

The Interdiction Car;maign . 
Th~~ VC force has reportedly increased 
by 20 ba.ttalions (from "(~.c to - 9[~) sinc:e last December, NVA by !~3 (from 
1~3 to 86) during the same perioJ. 
~~lle i\'VA represented only 25,600 of 
249,'(00 (10%) last December, in~ ~ ' ~a:::ing to L~5,6oo of 277,000 (16% in

The weekly rate of accepted infiltration has been about 1115 
in 1966 compared to 945 in 4th quarter 1965 and 510 for all of 1965 . 
MACV has recently reported that infiltration may have been as high as 
1630 per week in 1966 . 
The NVA unit s , equipped almost exclus i vely "li th 
Chinese and Russian "reapons, have a much greater requirement for infil-
trated aWJaunition and supplies, thus increasing their dependence on the

Air Interdiction. 
The use of air pOvrer to interdict enemy infiltration 
and supply has been very great by any standard. 
Attack sorties in ~~os and 
NVN ha;ve risen from 4750 per month at the end of last year to 9100 in 1st 
quarter of this year and to 10,600 and 12,900 in subsequent quarters. 
The 
j.nterdiction campaign has a.bsorbed most of the increase in deployed attack-
capable aircra~t in the past years. 
A substantial air interdiction campa.ign is clearly necessary and vlorth-
while. 
In addition to putting a ceiling on the size of the force that can 
be supported, it yields three significant military effects . 
First, it ef-
fectively harasses and delays truck movements dOHn through the southern 
panhandles of NVN and Laos, though it has no effect on troops infiltrating 
on foot over trails that are virtually invisible from the air. 
Our ex-
perience sho,-1s tha,t daytime armed reconnaissance above some minimum sortie 
rate makes it prohibitively expensive to the enemy to attempt daylight 
movement of vehicles , and so forces him to night movement . 
Second, des-
truction of bridges and cratering of roads forces the enemy to deploy repair 
crevls, equipmeilt, and porters to repajr or bypass the damage . 
Third, attacks 
on vehicles, parks, and r est camps destroy some vehicles v1i th their cargo~s 
and inflict casualties . 
Moreover, our bombing campaign may produce a bene-
ficial effect on U. S. and SVN morale by making NVN pay a price for its 
aggression and by shovring that v1e are doing vlhat we can to interdict the 
enemy. 
But at the scale i'le are nO"l operating, I believe our bombing is 
yielding very small marginal returns, not worth the cost in pilot lives 
and aj_rcraft .

In view of the ARVN ' s low efficiency in maj or combat operations and 
the increasing difficulties that SVN forces have had in recruiting and 
retaining the ~lanned forces in an overtaxed economy, I believe that we 
should not increase the SVN forces (ARVN, Regional and Popular Forces ) 
above the present strength of 158 battalions with 610,000 men . 
It is 
likely that GVN control can be extended most rapidly by using SVN forces 
mainly for revolutionary development, and using additional recruitable 
personnel for non-military and para-military revolutionary development 
duty . 
The ARVN must be retrained and assigned to RD duty, and General 
Hestmoreland plans to do so . 
The performance of the ARVN and other SVN 
forces as an instrument for winning popular support for the GVN has been 
decidedly unsatisfactory. 
Apparently ARVN personnel have not appreciated 
the decisive importance of revolutionary development and popular support; 
the importance of thesei terns 1-Till be heavily emphasized in the retraining 
programs .

Strategic Concept. 
(1)

rently.

Division and 173rd Airborne Brigade . 
By the time ATTLEBORO "las termin-
ated in late November , the enemy had lost over 1,000 killed . 
The 
pattern in III Corps , with the exception of a couple of operations in 
Phuoc Tuy Province designed basically to clear the lines of communica-
t ion from Saigon to Vung Tau, was a gradual shifting of emphasis north-
ward from Long An Province to Roa Ninh Province to Binh Duong and then 
north and west into Tay Ninh F-.covince and Har Zone C. §2/ 
By the end of the year , MACV estimated the total forces available 
to the enemy in Vietnam at 152 combat battalions, the total personnel 
strength of 280,600, of "Thich 123,600 Here comba,t or support troops, 
112,000 \·Tere militia, and 39,000 \'Ter e political cadre .

a figure of 48,400 infiltrators during the year .

true .

the first 6 months of 1966 vTas approximately 15 battalion eCluivalents .

year \'rore on. 81/

What Did It Mean? 
Program 4 had important historical antecedents vrhich provide the 
basic texture of the decision-making on Program 5. 
The preceding 
sections have outlined the major themes and historical developments 
which projected into the succeeding program with telling effect. 
These 
can be briefly summari zed as follOli}"s: 
(1) 
A precedent, albeit a seemingly fragi l e one, of essen-
tially saying "no" to the COMUS1>tll..CV force requirements ">'ras established. 
Actually, DoD and the President "\i}"ere beginning to question the concept 
of operation for Vietnam vrhich had l ed to programs , now becoming increas -
i ngly costly and depressingly barren of tangible results. 
The illusion 
of quick victory "on the cheap" had fled, and hard reality i ntervened . 
People in and out of government were beginning to seek alternatives to 
our policies in Vietnam vri th increased interest , and F-.cogram 5 was to 
increasingly reflect this basic mood surfacing in l ate 1966. 
(2) 
The JCS had ad.opted a strategic concept based upon ">'ridely 
expanded operations in the North, widened and intensified operations in 
t he South designed to seek out and destroy enemy forces, and committed 
to assisting the GVN in building e.n "independent, vi able, non- conununist 
societi' -- a vestige of the unfortunate wording of NSAM 288 . §]J 
The 
military heads had been denied the troops they said they required to 
successfully accomplish the objectives developed under the concept, but 
t he concept itself had survived. 
This strategic thought was to provide 
t he conceptual baseline for F-.cogram 5. 
(3) 
The basic troop requirement numbers , so i mportant to 
Program 5, ">,Tere introduced during Program 4. 
In fact, the refined 
figure the JCS proposed in JCSM 702-66 for mid-1968, 524,288, became 
t he eventue.l "approved" figure for Program 5. 
This number remained a 
focal point throughout the planning period despite frequent impor tant 
changes in the strategic situation . 
(4) 
Certain "oblique alternatives, " those vrhich were not 
directly substituta;ble options appeared during this time -- all of them 
designed to relieve pressure on U. S. resources, especially manpo\·rer. 
Among these weI'e the barrier plan (proposed by McNamaI'a ), ne"\"T free 
world military force sharing formulas (KANZUS), efforts to subtly hold 
the RVNAF ' s "feet to the fire ," and operations of various kinds i n the 
"sanctuaries ." 
(5) 
The Reserve mobilization line - - a pol itical sound ba.:crier 
as it \'rere, remained unbroken. 
The JCS had made a two-pronged case for bI'eaking it: One, 
the.t 
\'le could not adequately meet CINCPAC I s 1967 requirements and simul-
taneously fulfill our commitments to j\1A.TO and other threatened areas 
">'rithout mobili zation (a,nd even then probably de.ngerously late); and

any hope of success, the Combined Campaign Plan for 1967 r equired both

1:7here pacifica.tion was proceeding, and a If shelter
ll under which pacified 
areas could be r espectably kept that ,-lay. 
The II shieldlf concept could be 
implemented in a number of ''lays, statically or dynamically, (mobile vs . 
position defense ) geographically oriented or enemy force oriented, or by 
different combinations of these at different times . 
General Westmoreland ' s 
strategy based upon exploitation of our inherent superior mobility and 
firepovler "laS designed to simultaneously attrite the enemy and retain the 
initiative by disrupting VC/ NVA operations before they completely materi -
alized . 
This l ed to seeking engagement with enemy main force units well ' 
out into the border regions, where they literally could be held at distance 
before jmnping off in operations. 
Related to this was the notion that the 
i mportant thing vlaS to fight -- to engage the enemy and create casualties . 
It mattered little that you accepted combat in regions "Ti th certain 
advantg,ges for the enemy -- the prime objective was to engage and to kill 
him. 
Fighting the mobile defense kind of VTar provides an adequate but 
not perfect shield. 
You can liken it to a vast semi-permeable membrane 
,·rhich has significant l eakage by small amounts , over time . 
Backing up this 
kind of a II shieldlf is the If shel terll also manned by combat troops, geo-
graphically dispersed (actually occupying) in the areas where pacification 
is going on . 
The combination of the hTO, shield and shelter, require 
men and the balance is crucial, especially so if you have limited resources. 
If your operating assumptions are those held by CO~IDS~~CV in late 1966, 
then what you have for the II shelter lf is a function of the kind and sizes 
of enemy forces you are f ighting in the 11 shield
ll mission . If you are 
fighting large units at many points simultaneously, you are forced to 
strip II shelterlf forces -- or to use ARVN (or request more U. S, forces) . 
As Program 4 closes we find MACV facing just those same l arge multiple 
threat s , stripping the Ifshelterlf forces, and relying upon an inadequate 
ARVN for the maj ori ty of pacifice,tion security, 
'Hi th sufficient forces , 
U. S. and ARVN, the task ,vas prodigious -- and precarious. 
To attempt to 
If shieldlf 'Hi thout adequate forces to If shelterlf 'Has bound to be precarious .
